Truthfulness: An Obligatory Attribute of Prophets
Definition of prophetic truthfulness and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of truthfulness for prophets and the twofold proof (Quranic and logical) that lying is impossible for them.

Core Idea
Prophetic truthfulness is proven by both scripture and reason; a miracle from God cannot confirm a liar.

Sanusi establishes the second obligatory attribute for prophets—truthfulness (al-sidq). He defines it as the correspondence of their reports to reality, and then provides two proofs: a verse from the Quran and a rational argument based on the impossibility of God confirming a liar through miracles. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of scriptural and rational proof side by side.
2
Identify the definition and the two proofs
Find the definition of sidq and then locate the two proofs: the Quranic verse and the logical argument. Notice how the logical argument builds on the impossibility of God confirming a liar.
3
Understand the logical chain
Trace the conditional chain: if lying were possible for prophets, then God's confirmation via miracles would be lying, which is impossible. Therefore, lying must be impossible for prophets.
This is a foundational argument in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم النبوات
[ الواجب الثاني الصدق معناه، وبيان دلیل وجوبه ]
(و) من الواجب في حقهم (صِدْقُهُمْ ) (۱) أي : مطابقةُ حكم خبَرِهم (١) للواقع ، إيجابًا أو سلبا ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَصَدَقَ اللَّهُ وَرَسُولُهُ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ۲۲] . ولأنَّه لو جازَ عليهم الكذب لجاز الكذب في خبره تعالى؛ لتصديقه إيَّاهم بالمُعجزة النَّازِلِة منزلة قوله تعالى : صدق عبدي في كُلِّ ما يُبلغ عنِّي وتصديقُ الكاذب مِن العَالِم بكَذِبه محض كَذب، وهو محال عليه تعالى ؛
فملزومه - وهو جواز الكذب عليهم - كذلك .
(۱) قوله: (صدقهم) اعلم أن الصدق يتعلق تارة بـ: ۱ ـ دعوى الرسالة ، ٢ دعوى الرسالة ، ۲ - وتارة بدعوى الأحوال الشرعية ٣ - وتارة تتعلق بأحوال الناس كقام زيد وقام خالد، فالأقسام ثلاثة، والمقصود هنا الأولان ، وأما الثالث فهو داخل في الأمانة ، ولو التفت لعموم الأمانة لتضمن
جميع
ما بعدها. الشنواني.
Section of Prophethood. [The second obligation: truthfulness, its meaning and exposition of the proof of its obligation] (And) from the obligations with respect to them (is their truthfulness) (1) i.e. the correspondence of the content of their report (1) to reality, affirmatively or negatively; because of His statement, Exalted: 'And Allah and His Messenger are truthful' [Al-Ahzab 22]. And because if lying were permissible for them, then lying would be permissible in the news of God, Exalted; because of His confirming them with the miracle which takes the place of His saying: 'My servant is truthful in everything he conveys from Me.' And confirming a liar by one who knows his lie is pure lying, and it is impossible with respect to God, Exalted; so its entailment—which is the permissibility of lying for them—is likewise [impossible]. (1) His statement: (their truthfulness) – know that truthfulness relates sometimes to: 1. the claim of messengership, 2. the claim of messengership? Actually it mentions three: 1- claim of prophethood? The text reads: '1- دعوى الرسالة, 2- دعوى الرسالة,' which seems an error; but in the footnote: 'تارة بـ: ۱ ـ دعوى الرسالة ، ٢ دعوى الرسالة' – it is corrected later: 'الأقسام ثلاثة' and mentions 'الأولان' meaning first two. However the footnote says: '1. claim of messengership, 2. claim of legal rulings? Actually it says: '۱ ـ دعوى الرسالة ، ٢ دعوى الرسالة' which is likely a typo; the intended categories are: 1. claim of prophethood, 2. claim of legal rulings, 3. reports about people. The author says the first two are meant here, and the third is included in trustworthiness. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage defines the second obligatory attribute: truthfulness (al-sidq). The definition is the correspondence of their reports to reality, whether affirmative or negative. The proof is twofold: a Quranic verse (al-Ahzab 22) and a logical argument. The logical argument states: if lying were possible for prophets, then God's confirming them through miracles would amount to God affirming a lie, which is impossible because God knows the truth. Therefore, lying must be impossible for them. The footnote expands on the types of truthfulness: three categories—claim of prophethood (or messengership), claim of legal rulings, and reports about worldly matters. The first two are the ones relevant here; the third is already covered under trustworthiness. Note: The Arabic in the footnote appears to have a possible duplication error in the enumeration (two times 'دعوى الرسالة'), but the intended meaning is clear from the explanation.

Key Terms
الصدق
المعجزة
تصديق الكاذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Truthfulness for prophets is a moral virtue but not logically necessary.
Correction: The logical proof shows it is impossible for prophets to lie because God's confirmation would then be lying.

Checkpoint
According to the logical proof in the passage, why is lying impossible for prophets?
A) Because they are naturally honest.
B) Because God would not confirm a liar with miracles.
C) Because the Quran says so implicitly.
D) Because people would not believe them.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic truthfulness is obligatory and rationally necessary; without it, divine truthfulness would be compromised.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever speak about Islam without verifying the truth of your words, even in small matters? How does the Prophet's absolute truthfulness challenge you to be exact in every word you say about the religion?
?
If God cannot confirm a liar, what does it mean that you claim to follow the Prophet while your actions contradict his teachings? Are you living in a way that confirms or betrays the truthfulness of his message?
?
When you misrepresent an Islamic ruling to benefit yourself, you are effectively attributing a lie to God and His Prophet. Will you repent and commit to truthfulness starting now?

The Three Categories of Prophetic Truthfulness
Enumeration of the three types of prophetic truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of truthfulness related to prophets: claims of messengership, claims of legal rulings, and reports about worldly matters.

Core Idea
Prophetic truthfulness covers messages from God, religious rulings, and worldly reports.

In the commentary on truthfulness, al-Shanwani clarifies that sidq is not monolithic. It applies to three distinct domains: the prophet's claim to be a messenger, his statements about religious laws, and his everyday reports. This lesson explains those categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote carefully
Read the Arabic footnote and its translation. Note the three categories enumerated.
There is a duplication in the Arabic, but the intended three are clear from context.
2
Identify the three categories
List the three types: claim of prophethood, claim of legal rulings, and reports about people (worldly matters).
3
Understand why the third is under amanah
The commentator says the third category (worldly reports) is covered under the attribute of trustworthiness (al-amanah), not truthfulness proper.
This shows the interconnectedness of prophetic attributes.

The Obligatory Attribute of Intelligence (al-Fatanah) for Prophets
Definition of al-fatanah with proof and objection response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-fatanah, the proof for its obligation, and why it applies to all prophets, not only messengers.

Core Idea
Intelligence to refute falsehood is required for every prophet, not just messengers.

In this lesson, we examine the third obligatory attribute for prophets: intelligence (al-fatanah). The author defines it as acute perception needed to counter opponents and invalidate false claims. A key nuance emerges in the footnotes: while the text suggests it may be specific to messengers, the commentator clarifies it is general for all prophets. We will explore the proof from Quran and the objection to generalization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and the English translation side by side. Notice the flow from definition to the apparent view, then the footnote correction, and finally the proof with objection.
Pay attention to the footnote numbers—they contain crucial clarifications.
2
Understand the meaning of al-fatanah
The passage defines al-fatanah as acute perception (al-tafattun) and alertness for compelling opponents. Note that it is more specific than general understanding.
3
Identify the apparent vs. corrected view
The text initially says 'the apparent is that this obligation is specific to messengers.' But footnote (3) corrects this, arguing it is general because even non-messenger prophets need intelligence to refute opponents.
The commentator also offers a second opinion: the condition for prophethood is absolute intelligence, while for messengers it is perfection of intelligence.
4
Examine the proof and objection
The proof is a Qur'anic reference ('And that is Our argument which We gave'). An objection is raised: the verses only apply to some prophets. The answer is that what establishes perfection for one prophet applies to all, as prophethood is a single station.
This is a standard kalam argument from uniformity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الواجب الثالث: الفطانة معناها، ودليل وجوبها] (وَضِفْ) أي: وضُمِّ (لَهُ) أي: لما يجبُّ لهم (الفَطَانَهُ) بمعنى التَّفطن (۲) والتيقظ لإلزام الخصوم وحِجَاجِهم ، وطرق إبطال دعاويهم الباطلة. والظاهر اختصاص هذا الواجب بالرُّسل (۳) ؛ لقوله تعالى (٤) وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا ءَاتَيْنَهَا :
(۲) قوله: (بمعنى التفطن التفطن هو : إدراك الأمور الدقيقة ، وهو أخص من الفهم. الشنواني.
(۳) هذا خلاف الظاهر ، بل هو عام ، لأنهم وإن لم يكونوا رُسلا لأحدٍ ؛ لكن عندهم من والذكاء ما يَردُّون به الخصم ويفحمونه ، على تقدير وقوع جدال منهم، وهو الأليق النبوة ، إلا أن يقال: إن المُشترط في النبوة مطلق الفطنة ، بخلاف الرُّسل فإنّ المُشترط فيهم
بمنصب
کمال الفطنة. الشنواني.
(٤) قد يُعترض : أن هذه الآيات التي سيذكرها الشارح ليست واردة إلا في بعضهم، فكيف عممتُموها على الجميع ؟ يُجاب: أنَّ ما ثبت لواحد مما به كماله يثبت لغيره، لأنه لا فرق
بين مقام نبوة أحدهم ونبوة نبي آخر من حيث النَّبوة.
[The third obligation: intelligence, its meaning and the proof of its obligation] (And add) i.e. and join (to it) i.e. to what is obligatory for them (al-fatanah) meaning acute perception (2) and alertness for compelling opponents and their arguments, and ways of invalidating their false claims. And the apparent is that this obligation is specific to messengers (3); because of His statement, Exalted: 'And that is Our argument which We gave' (4). (2) His statement: (meaning acute perception) – acute perception is the grasp of subtle matters, and it is more specific than understanding. Al-Shanwani. (3) This is contrary to the apparent, rather it is general, because even if they are not messengers to anyone, they still possess sharpness and intelligence with which they refute the opponent and silence him, assuming that debate occurs from them, and that is more befitting of prophethood, unless it is said: The condition for prophethood is absolute intelligence, unlike messengers, for the condition for them is perfection of intelligence. Al-Shanwani. (4) It may be objected: These verses that the commentator will mention are only reported concerning some of them, so how did you generalize them to all? It is answered: What is established for one of them which constitutes his perfection is established for others, because there is no difference between the station of prophethood of any of them and the prophethood of another prophet in terms of prophethood.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage defines the third obligatory attribute: intelligence (al-fatanah). It means acute perception and alertness necessary for compelling opponents and refuting false arguments. The author notes that the apparent view is that this obligation is specific to messengers, but the footnote corrects this: it is general for all prophets, because even non-messenger prophets are expected to have such intelligence to defend the truth. The proof is a reference to Quranic verses (the text says 'And that is Our argument which We gave'—likely referring to Abraham). The fourth footnote addresses an objection: the verses mentioned may only apply to some prophets, but the answer is that what is established for one prophet regarding perfection applies to all because prophethood is one station.

Key Terms

Misconception: Intelligence (al-fatanah) is only required for messengers who debate against opponents.
Correction: It is required for all prophets because they must be able to refute false claims, even if not sent as messengers.

Checkpoint

The Obligatory Attribute of Truthfulness for Prophets
Definition and proof of the second obligatory attribute for prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of prophetic truthfulness (sidq) and the rational proof that establishes it as an obligatory attribute.

Core Idea
Prophetic truthfulness is proven by the impossibility of Allah confirming a liar.

The second obligatory attribute for prophets is truthfulness (sidq). This lesson unpacks its precise definition and the compelling Quranic and rational evidence that makes it a necessary quality. You will see how the rational argument ties prophetic truthfulness directly to Allah's own veracity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the source text once without trying to analyze. Then read the English translation. Let the author's structure come through.
Notice the footnote in parentheses—it clarifies the types of sidq.
2
Understand the definition
Sidq is defined as 'the conformity of the judgment of their report with reality'. Contrast this with common notions of truthfulness as mere sincerity.
The definition is broad: it covers both positive and negative reports.
3
Examine the rational proof
Follow the logical chain: If prophets could lie, then Allah's confirmation of them via miracles would be Allah lying—which is impossible. Therefore, lying is impossible for prophets.
This is a conditional argument: the impossibility of the consequence shows the impossibility of the antecedent.
4
Consider the footnote on types
The footnote lists three types of sidq: relating to prophethood, to legal rulings, and to ordinary reports. The first two are the focus here; the third falls under trustworthiness.
This shows that sidq is not monolithic but applies to different domains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) من الواجب في حقهم (صِدْقُهُمْ ) (۱) أي : مطابقةُ حكم خَبَرِهم (١) للواقع ، إيجابًا أو سلبا ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَصَدَقَ اللَّهُ وَرَسُولُهُ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ۲۲] . ولأنَّه لو جازَ عليهم الكذب لجاز الكذب في خبره تعالى؛ لتصديقه إيَّاهم بالمُعجزة النَّازِلِة منزلة قوله تعالى : صدق عبدي في كُلِّ ما يُبلغ عنِّي وتصديقُ الكاذِب مِن العَالِم بكَذِبه محض كَذب، وهو محال عليه تعالى ؛ فملزومه - وهو جواز الكذب عليهم - كذلك . (۱) قوله: (صدقهم) اعلم أن الصدق يتعلق تارة بـ: ۱ ـ دعوى الرسالة ، ٢ دعوى الرسالة ، ۲ - وتارة بدعوى الأحوال الشرعية ٣ - وتارة تتعلق بأحوال الناس كقام زيد وقام خالد، فالأقسام ثلاثة، والمقصود هنا الأولان ، وأما الثالث فهو داخل في الأمانة ، ولو التفت لعموم الأمانة لتضمن جميع ما بعدها. الشنواني.
(And) from the obligatory in their right is their truthfulness (sidquhum). That is: the conformity of the judgment of their report with reality, positively or negatively. Due to His saying, exalted: 'And Allah and His Messenger were truthful.' And because if lying were possible for them, then lying would be possible in the news of Allah, exalted, because of His confirming them by the miracle which is in the position of His saying, exalted: 'My servant is truthful in everything he conveys from Me.' And confirming a liar by one who knows his lie is sheer lying, and that is impossible for Allah, exalted; so its necessary condition — which is the possibility of lying for them — is likewise. (1) His saying: (their truthfulness). Know that truthfulness is sometimes related to the claim of prophethood, sometimes to the claim of legal rulings, and sometimes related to people's affairs like 'Zayd stood' and 'Khalid stood'. So the categories are three, and the intended here are the first two. As for the third, it is included in trustworthiness (al-amanah). And if attention were given to the generality of trustworthiness, it would encompass all that follows. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage defines the obligatory attribute of truthfulness (sidq) for prophets and provides its proof. It begins with the definition: sidq means that the judgment contained in the prophet's report matches reality, whether it is affirmative or negative. Then it gives two proofs. First, a Quranic proof: the verse 'And Allah and His Messenger were truthful.' Second, a rational proof: if lying were possible for the prophets, then lying would be possible in the news of Allah Himself. This is because Allah confirms the prophet's truthfulness through miracles; the miracle serves as Allah's statement that the prophet is truthful in conveying from Him. Confirming a liar while knowing him to be a liar would itself be lying, which is impossible for Allah. Therefore, its necessary condition — that lying is possible for prophets — is also impossible. The footnote then clarifies the types of sidq: it can be related to the claim of prophethood, to legal rulings, or to ordinary reports. The focus here is on the first two types; the third falls under the attribute of trustworthiness (amanah).

Key Terms
صدق
مطابقة للواقع
معجزة
أمانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sidq only means 'not lying' or 'being truthful in the sense of sincerity'.
Correction: Sidq is specifically defined as the conformity of the report with reality (mutaabaqah lil-waaqi'), not just a general disposition. It is also distinct from amanah (trustworthiness).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rational proof for the obligation of truthfulness in prophets?
A) Allah says 'And Allah and His Messenger were truthful'
B) If prophets could lie, then Allah would be confirming a liar through miracles, which is impossible for Allah.
C) Prophets are infallible by consensus.
D) The definition of sidq requires it.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic truthfulness is not just a moral virtue but a necessary attribute proven by the impossibility of Allah confirming a liar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your acceptance of every prophetic report were truly tested, which report—whether from the Quran or hadith—do you find yourself most hesitant to fully embrace? After this lesson, how will you handle that hesitation?
?
The proof shows that Allah's confirming a liar is impossible. Reflect on a time you doubted a prophetic statement because it seemed hard to accept. How does the knowledge that every prophetic report is absolutely true and backed by Allah's own truthfulness change your posture toward that statement? What specific action will you take to submit to it?
?
Al-Shanwani's footnote says ordinary reports fall under amanah. This means even the smallest statement of the prophet—like 'today is Friday'—is trustworthy. Can you recall a specific instance where you dismissed a prophetic saying as 'just a minor detail'? How will you treat such reports from now on, knowing that the prophet's truthfulness covers all his speech?

The Obligatory Attribute of Intelligence (Fatanah) for Prophets
Defines fatanah and gives proof from Quran and reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of fatanah (intelligence) as an obligatory attribute for prophets, its proofs from the Quran and reason, and the distinction that it is required for all prophets, though in a perfect degree for messengers.

Core Idea
Prophets are God's witnesses; they must possess sharp intelligence to refute falsehood.

This lesson examines the third obligatory attribute for prophets: intelligence (fatanah). After defining it, the author presents both scriptural and rational arguments establishing that prophets must possess acute intelligence to fulfill their role as witnesses against mankind. The passage also addresses a subtle point about whether this attribute is exclusive to messengers or common to all prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the definition of fatanah and the proofs that follow.
The passage includes footnotes that expand on key points.
2
Identify the two types of proof
The author provides both Quranic verses and a rational argument. Find them and see how they support the necessity of fatanah.
The rational argument is concise: a witness cannot be foolish.
3
Understand the scope of the attribute
Note the apparent specification to messengers, but the footnote clarifies it is general for all prophets. Then see how the objection and answer justify this generalization.
This shows the principle that what is proven for one prophet regarding essential attributes applies to all.

(And add) i.e., join (to it) i.e., to what is obligatory for them (al-fatanah) meaning acumen and alertness to refute opponents and their arguments, and ways of invalidating their false claims. And the apparent is that this obligation is specific to messengers (3); because of His saying (4) 'And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people' [6:83], 'O Noah, you have disputed with us' [11:32], and 'And argue with them in a way that is best' [16:125]. Commentary
This passage defines and proves the obligatory attribute of intelligence (fatanah) for prophets. The Obligatory Attribute of Delivery (Tabligh) for Prophets
Defines tabligh and proves its obligation using consensus and Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation of prophetic delivery (tabligh), its proof from consensus and the Prophet's own practice, and the nuance that prophets are only required to deliver what they are commanded to convey.

Core Idea
Prophets must deliver all they are commanded to, and concealment is impossible for them.

This lesson turns to the fourth attribute that every prophet must possess: the complete and fearless delivery of the divine message. The author grounds this obligation in both rational reasoning and scriptural evidence, showing that concealment — even under fear — is impossible for prophets. He also clarifies that not everything received from Allah is to be publicly delivered; some matters are commanded to be concealed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how the author first states the obligation, then provides evidence.
The passage includes a footnote that adds an important clarification about categories of divine communication.
2
Identify the proof from the Prophet's example
Find the rational proof: if concealment were possible, the Prophet would have concealed the verse about his fear of people, but he did not. This proves that concealment is impossible for all prophets.
This argument is based on an incident in Surah al-Ahzab (33:37).
3
Understand the three categories of divine communications
The footnote explains that not everything from Allah must be delivered: there are commands to convey, commands to conceal, and matters left to choice. This refines the definition of tabligh.
This nuance prevents misunderstandings that prophets must reveal all divine secrets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦٠ - وَمِثْلُ ذَا تَبْلِيغُهُمْ لما أَتَوا وَيَسْتَحِيلُ ضِدُّها كَمَا رَوَوْا (وَمِثْلُ ذَا ) أي : الواجب المُتقدِّم في الوجوب العقلي في حقِّ الرُّسل الصَّلاة والسَّلام تَبْلِيغُهُمْ لِمَا أَتَوْا) أي: لجميع ما جاءوا به من عند الله وأرسلوا لتبليغه للعباد (۱) ، فيجب شرعاً اعتقاد أنَّهم بلغوه إليهم، اعتقاديًّا كان أو عمليًّا ؛ للإجماع على عصمتهم من كتمان الرّسالة والتقصير في التبليغ ، ولو في قوَّة الخوف، ولو جاز عليهم كتمان شيء لكتَمَ رئيسهم الأعظم الله وعليهم .. قوله تعالى: ﴿وَتُخْفِي فِي نَفْسِكَ مَا اللَّهُ مُبْدِيهِ وَتَخْشَى النَّاسَ وَاللَّهُ أَحَقُّ أَن تَخشَه ) [الأحزاب: ٣٧](٢). كيف وقد أُنزِلَ عليه: ﴿يَتَأَيُّهَا الرَّسُولُ بَلِّغْ مَا أُنزِلَ (۱) قيده بتبليغ ما أرسلوا بتبليغه للعباد ؛ لأنه لا يجب عليهم تبليغ كل ما بلغهم من الله ، لأنَّ الأقسام ثلاثة: ۱ - ما أمر بتبليغه . ۲ - ما أمر بكتمه . ٣ ـ وما خُيّر فيه. قال العلامة البيضاوي في تفسير قوله تعالى: بَلَغَ مَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْكَ مِن رَّبِّكَ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] لعل المراد بتبليغه ما يتعلق بمصالح العباد وقُصِدَ بإنزاله اطلاعهم عليه ، لا تبليغ كل ما نزل ، فإنَّ مِن الأسرار الإلهية ما يحرُم إفشاؤه. (۲) اعلم أنَّ أصح محامله نقل من يُعول عليه في التفسير عن علي بن الحسين: «من أنَّ الله تعالى=
60 — And similar to this is their delivery of what they brought, and its opposite is impossible as they narrated. (And similar to this) i.e., the obligation preceding in rational obligation regarding the messengers, prayers and peace be upon them, is their delivery of what they brought, i.e., all that they came with from Allah and were sent to deliver to the servants (1). So it is legally obligatory to believe that they delivered it to them, whether it is belief-based or practice-based, due to the consensus on their infallibility from concealing the message and falling short in delivery, even from fear. And if it were possible for them to conceal something, then their greatest chief would have concealed it — Allah and upon them... His saying, exalted: 'And you concealed within yourself what Allah was to disclose, and you feared people, while Allah is more deserving that you fear Him' [33:37] (2). How, when it was revealed to him: 'O Messenger, convey what was revealed...' (1) He restricted it to delivering what they were sent to deliver to the servants, because it is not obligatory for them to deliver everything that came to them from Allah, for the categories are three: 1 - what they are commanded to deliver, 2 - what they are commanded to conceal, 3 - and what they are given a choice in. The scholar al-Baydawi said in the interpretation of His saying: 'Convey what was revealed to you from your Lord' [5:67]: perhaps the intended is delivering that which concerns the interests of the servants and its revelation intended their knowledge of it, not delivery of everything revealed, for among divine secrets are those which it is forbidden to disclose. (2) Know that the most correct interpretation, transmitted by one relied upon in tafsir from Ali ibn al-Husayn: 'That Allah, exalted...'
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the obligatory attribute of delivery (tabligh) for messengers. It begins by referencing the poem line, stating that like the previous obligations, delivery of what they brought is also obligatory, and its opposite (concealment) is impossible. The commentary explains that 'delivery of what they brought' means all that they came with from Allah and were sent to deliver to the servants. It is obligatory to believe firmly that they delivered everything, whether it is belief-related or practice-related, based on the consensus that prophets are infallible from concealing the message or falling short in delivery, even under fear. The rational proof: if it were possible for them to conceal something, then the greatest of them (the Prophet Muhammad) would have concealed the verse 'And you concealed within yourself what Allah was to disclose, and you feared people, while Allah is more deserving that you fear Him.' But he did not conceal it, so it is impossible for any prophet to conceal. The footnotes add: (1) clarifies that delivery is only of what they were commanded to deliver; there are three categories: what is commanded to deliver, what is commanded to conceal, and what is optional. The verse 'Convey what was revealed' is interpreted to mean delivering what concerns the interests of the servants. (2) begins an explanation of the verse, but the text cuts off.

عصمة (infallibility)
كتمان (concealment)

Misconception: Some think prophets must deliver every single thing they receive from Allah, even divine secrets.
Correction: The passage clarifies that prophets are only required to deliver what they are commanded to convey; some matters are commanded to be concealed, and some are left to their choice.

Checkpoint

A) Consensus of Muslims.
B) If concealment were possible, the Prophet Muhammad would have concealed the verse about his own fear of people, but he did not.
C) Quranic verse 'O Messenger, convey what was revealed'.
D) The definition of tabligh requires it.

Key Takeaway
Prophets must deliver all they are commanded to deliver, and their infallibility from concealment is proven by both consensus and the fact that the Prophet Muhammad did not conceal even a personally difficult verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the Prophet's example of reciting the verse about Zaynab despite his fear expose your own weakness? Will you commit today to delivering a hard truth you have been avoiding?
?
You believe the prophets delivered everything without concealment. But do you live as if you also must deliver every command of Allah without concealment, or do you pick and choose based on how it will be received? What one command are you currently 'concealing' in your life, and what is the real reason? What would it cost you to deliver it fully?
?
The lesson teaches that prophets are infallible from concealment, but you are not. When you fail to speak the truth or water down a divine command, are you comfortable with that? If the Prophet could not hide even a personally difficult verse, why do you feel entitled to hide the truth in your own interactions? What step will you take today to align your speech with your belief in tabligh?

The Fourth Obligatory Belief: Messengers Must Convey
Al-Sanusi defines the fourth wajib and proves it with consensus and Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the fourth obligatory belief about the prophets is that they conveyed everything they were commanded to convey, and why concealing any part is impossible for them.

Core Idea
Prophets are infallible in conveying the message; they cannot hide anything.

Al-Sanusi now turns to the fourth wajib regarding the messengers: the obligation to believe that they conveyed everything they were commanded to convey. This belief is rooted in the consensus that prophets are ma'sum (infallible) from concealing or falling short in delivery. The passage explains why this is necessary and supports it with Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the poetic line and the commentary that follows.
The passage begins with the line 'وَمِثْلُ ذَا تَبْلِيغُهُمْ...'
2
Identify the fourth wajib and its proof
Locate where Al-Sanusi states the obligation and where he provides the proof from consensus (ijma') that prophets are infallible from concealment.
The proof is 'للإجماع على عصمتهم من كتمان الرسالة والتقصير في التبليغ'
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the three Quranic verses cited. Understand how each one supports the necessity of complete conveyance and the impossibility of concealment.
Verses from Al-Ahzab, Al-Ma'idah, and An-Nisa are quoted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجب الرابع: اعتقاد تبليغ الرسل ما أمروا بتبليغه
٦٠ - وَمِثْلُ ذَا تَبْلِيغُهُمْ لما أَتَوا وَيَسْتَحِيلُ ضِدُها كَمَا رَوَوْا
(وَمِثْلُ ذَا) أي: الواجب المتقدم في الوجوب العقلي في حق الرسل الصلاة والسلام تبليغهم لما أتوا أي: لجميع ما جاءوا به من عند الله وأرسلوا لتبليغه للعباد، فيجب شرعاً اعتقاد أنهم بلغوه إليهم، اعتقادياً كان أو عملياً؛ للإجماع على عصمتهم من كتمان الرسالة والتقصير في التبليغ، ولو في قوة الخوف، ولو جاز عليهم كتمان شيء لكتَمَ رئيسهم الأعظم الله وعليهم .. قوله تعالى: ﴿وَتُخْفِي فِي نَفْسِكَ مَا اللَّهُ مُبْدِيهِ وَتَخْشَى النَّاسَ وَاللَّهُ أَحَقُّ أَن تَخشَه) [الأحزاب: ٣٧]. كيف وقد أُنزل عليه: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الرَّسُولُ بَلِّغْ مَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْكَ مِن رَّبِّكَ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] رُسُلاً مُبَشِّرِينَ وَمُنذِرِينَ لِئَلَّا يَكُونَ لِلنَّاسِ عَلَى اللَّهِ حُجَّةٌ بَعْدَ الرُّسُلِ) [النساء: ١٦٥]. وكتمان البعض مُفوِّت لإقامة الحجة.
The fourth obligation: belief that the messengers conveyed what they were commanded to convey. 60 - And similar to this is their conveyance of what they brought, and its opposite is impossible as they narrated. ('And similar to this') meaning: the obligation preceding in rational obligation regarding the messengers, prayers and peace be upon them, is their conveyance of what they brought, i.e., all that they came with from Allah and were sent to convey to the servants. So it is legally obligatory to believe that they conveyed it to them, whether it is belief-related or action-related, due to consensus on their infallibility from concealing the message and falling short in conveyance, even in the face of fear. And if concealment of anything were possible for them, then their greatest chief (PBUH) would have concealed ... Allah says: 'And you hide within yourself what Allah would reveal and fear the people, while Allah is more deserving that you fear Him' [Al-Ahzab: 37]. How so, when it was revealed to him: 'O Messenger, convey that which has been revealed to you from your Lord' [Al-Ma'idah: 67], 'messengers as bearers of good tidings and warners so that mankind will have no argument against Allah after the messengers' [An-Nisa: 165]. And concealing some would nullify the establishment of the proof.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth wajib (obligation) concerning the messengers: belief that they conveyed what they were commanded to convey. The commentator explains that the wajib mentioned in the preceding line refers to the rational obligation regarding the messengers. He then states that it is legally obligatory to believe that they conveyed all that they were sent with, whether it is doctrinal or practical. The proof is the consensus (ijma) that the prophets are infallible (ma'sum) from concealing the message or falling short in delivery, even under duress. He supports this with the Quranic verse about the Prophet hiding something in himself (referring to the Zaynab incident) but then commanded to convey. Another verse commands general conveyance, and the final verse states that messengers were sent as warners so that people have no excuse. Concealing even part would defeat the purpose of establishing the proof. Thus, the passage establishes the necessity of tabligh and the impossibility of kitman (concealment).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that prophets could conceal minor things out of fear or expediency.
Correction: The passage states that prophets are infallible from concealing the message even in the face of fear, and if concealment were possible, the Prophet (PBUH) would have concealed, but Allah rebuked him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it obligatory to believe that the messengers conveyed everything?

B) Because they were given special powers

Key Takeaway
The messengers conveyed everything they were commanded to convey, and it is impossible for them to conceal anything.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Prophet (peace be upon him) was rebuked for hiding something in his heart. What small truth about your own faith or practice are you hiding from those around you? When will you step out of that comfort zone and speak it?
?
If the messengers were infallible in conveying the message, what does that mean for you as a believer who is not infallible? What part of the message are you neglecting to convey to your family or friends, and what is your plan to start conveying it even partially?

The Classification of Prophethood Conditions
Al-Sharh classifies conditions of prophethood and enumerates legal/customary ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of conditions for prophethood and be able to list the specific conditions that fall under the legal and customary categories.

Core Idea
Prophethood requires both rational necessities and revealed conditions.

Al-Sharh expands on the conditions of prophethood beyond the purely rational. He introduces legal and customary conditions derived from revelation and divine practice. This lesson focuses on the classification and the list of six specific conditions that a prophet must possess.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three categories
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that al-Nazim (the author) only mentioned rational conditions, but the commentator adds legal and customary conditions. Identify what those three categories are.
2
List the six legal/customary conditions
The passage lists six conditions: humanity, freedom, masculinity, perfection of intellect, intelligence, and strength of opinion. Write them down and consider why each might be required for a prophet.
3
Reflect on the rationale behind these conditions
Ask yourself: why are these conditions considered 'legal' or 'customary' rather than purely rational? How do they relate to the function of a prophet in society?
This connects to the broader methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الشروط الشرعية والعادية للنبوة ]
وما ذكره النَّاظم رحمه الله تعالى شروط عقليَّة للنُّبوَّة، وشروطها الشَّرعيّة والعادية: البشريَّة، والحرّيَّة، والذكورة، وكمال العقل، والذكاء، وقوَّة الرّأي.
(1) الصواب أن الأمانة والتبليغ والفطانة دليلها شرعي، وأما الصدق في دعوى الرسالة والأحكام الشرعية فدليله عقلي، كما تقدم في نقلنا عن اليوسي في حاشيته على شرح كبرى السنوسي . فراجعه . الشنواني .
(2) جعلُ الذكاء من الشروط الشرعية، مع أن صاحب القاموس فسره: بسرعة الفطنة، أنَّ من الشروط العقلية مُشكل، وقد تقدم أن التحقيق أن الفطانة معنى له، له، لأنَّ القصد أن النبي لا [؟] الفطانة الشارح من الشروط الشرعية، وقول الشارح: (العادية) بعد قوله الشرعية مما لا يكون إلا متصفا بهذه الصفات، ففيه أن العادة لا تعتبر هنا، فإن أراد عادةَ الله في أنبيائه؛ رجع للشرعية. الأمير والشنواني.
(3) أي الادراك، فهو يرجع للذكاء، فكمال العقل والأمران بعده في كلام الشارح نفس الفطنة، =
[The legal and customary conditions of prophethood] And what the author (al-Nazim) mentioned, may Allah have mercy on him, are rational conditions for prophethood. And its legal and customary conditions are: humanity, freedom, masculinity, perfection of intellect, intelligence, and strength of opinion. (1) The correct view is that the evidence for trustworthiness, conveyance, and acumen is legal, while the evidence for truthfulness in claiming prophethood and legal rulings is rational, as previously mentioned in our quotation from al-Yusi in his gloss on the Sharh Kubra al-Sanusiyya. So consult it. - al-Shanwani. (2) Making intelligence one of the legal conditions, while the author of al-Qamus explained it as quickness of acumen, that it is from the rational conditions is problematic. And it has preceded that the verification is that acumen is a meaning for him, for him, because the intent is that the prophet does not [؟] acumen the commentator from the legal conditions. And the commentator's saying 'customary' after his saying 'legal' indicates that he is only described by these attributes. Then it includes that custom is not considered here. For if he intends Allah's custom with His prophets, it returns to being legal. - al-Amir and al-Shanwani. (3) That is, perception, so it returns to intelligence. So perfection of intellect and the two matters after it in the commentator's statement are the same as acumen. =
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage classifies the conditions of prophethood into three types: rational, legal, and customary. The author (al-Nazim) only mentioned rational conditions, but the commentator adds legal and customary ones. The legal and customary conditions listed are: humanity (bashariyya), freedom (hurriyya), masculinity (dhukura), perfect intellect (kamal al-aql), intelligence (dhaka'), and strength of opinion (quwwat al-ra'y). The footnote then discusses the nature of these conditions: the evidence for amana (trustworthiness), tabligh (conveyance), and fatana (acumen) is legal (shar'i), while the evidence for sidq (truthfulness) in claiming prophethood and delivering legal rulings is rational (aqli). Another footnote debates whether intelligence (dhaka') should be considered a legal or rational condition, referencing the lexicon and earlier discussions. The third footnote equates 'strength of opinion' with perception, which reduces to intelligence, thus considering perfection of intellect and the two subsequent items as essentially the same as acumen. The overall point is that prophethood has conditions beyond the purely rational, and these are derived from revelation and customary practice.

شرعي
عادي

قوة الرأي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All conditions of prophethood are purely rational and can be deduced by reason alone.
Correction: While some conditions are rational necessities (e.g., truthfulness), others like masculinity and freedom are derived from revelation (shar'i) or divine custom (adi).

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT listed as a legal or customary condition of prophethood in the passage?
A) Bashariyya
B) Dhukura
C) 'Isma (infallibility)
D) Kamal al-aql

Key Takeaway
The conditions of prophethood include rational necessities as well as legal and customary requirements derived from revelation and divine practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The condition of 'humanity' means the Prophet ﷺ experienced hunger, joy, and sorrow just like you. When you feel distant from him, does it help to remember that he was human? How can you this week imitate one of his human struggles (like patience in poverty) in your own life?
?
The passage includes 'strength of opinion' (quwwat al-ra'y) as a prophetic quality. In what area of your daily decisions do you waver because you fear people's opinions? What is one step you can take tonight to decisively choose what pleases Allah, even if it costs you popularity?

Evidence for Prophetic Attributes: Rational or Legal?
Al-Sharh discusses the evidential basis of prophetic conditions through three footnotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between rational and legal evidence for prophetic attributes and how the nature of evidence affects the classification of those attributes.

Core Idea
Some prophetic attributes are proven by reason, others only by revelation.

The footnotes in this passage delve into the epistemological basis of the conditions. The commentator explains that trustworthiness, conveyance, and acumen are proven by legal evidence (shar'i), while truthfulness in claiming prophethood is rational. This lesson unpacks these nuances and the debate around intelligence as a condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnotes alongside the commentary
Read the three footnotes carefully. They debate which conditions have rational evidence and which have legal evidence. Pay attention to the specific attributes mentioned: amana, tabligh, fatana, sidq, and dhaka'.
Footnote (2) has some textual difficulty but the core argument is clear.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
Note that the first footnote distinguishes between rational evidence (for sidq in da'wa and ahkam) and legal evidence (for amana, tabligh, fatana). Understand why this distinction matters.
3
Examine the debate on intelligence (dhaka')
The second footnote argues that intelligence should be a rational condition, not legal, based on its definition. Consider how definitions affect classification.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in kalam.
4
Reflect on the overall implications
Think about how this epistemological classification influences the methodology of proving prophethood. Why is it important to know which attributes are rationally necessary and which are legally required?

This passage classifies the conditions of prophethood into three types: rational, legal, and customary. The author (al-Nazim) only mentioned rational conditions, but the commentator adds legal and customary ones. The legal and customary conditions listed are: humanity (bashariyya), freedom (hurriyya), masculinity (dhukura), perfect intellect (kamal al-aql), intelligence (dhaka'), and strength of opinion (quwwat al-ra'y). Classification of Prophethood Conditions
Sanusi distinguishes rational conditions from legal/customary conditions of prophethood and lists the latter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between rational and legal/customary conditions of prophethood as presented by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Prophethood has conditions beyond belief: some are rationally necessary, others legally required.

Sanusi begins by categorizing the conditions of prophethood into two types: those that reason demands (rational) and those that divine law and custom require (legal/customary). This classification helps us understand the basis for each requirement. The passage lists the legal/customary conditions explicitly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysing. Note the two categories mentioned: rational and legal/customary.
The footnote numbers (1,2,3) indicate there are clarifications elsewhere.
2
Identify the listed conditions
The passage lists six conditions under the legal/customary category. Write them down mentally.
Compare with your own understanding of prophethood.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Think about why some conditions are called 'rational' and others 'legal/customary'. What might be the difference?
The commentary will later clarify that certain conditions have rational proof while others have legal proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره النَّاظم رحمه الله تعالى شروط عقليَّة للنُّبوَّة (١) ، وشروطها الشَّرعيّة والعادية: البشريَّة، والحرّيَّة ، والذكورة ، وكمال العقل ، والذكاء (٢) ، وقوَّة الرّأي (۳)
(١) الصواب أن الأمانة والتبليغ والفطانة دليلها شرعي ، وأما الصدق في دعوى الرسالة والأحكام الشرعية فدليله عقلي ، كما تقدم في نقلنا عن اليوسي في حاشيته على شرح كبرى السنوسي . فراجعه . الشنواني .
(۲) جعلُ الذكاء من الشروط الشرعية، مع أن صاحب القاموس فسره: بسرعة الفطنة، أنَّ من الشروط العقلية مُشكل، وقد تقدم أن التحقيق أن الفطانة معنى له ، له ، لأنَّ القصد أن النبي لا الفطانة الشارح من الشروط الشرعية ، وقول الشارح: (العادية) بعد قوله الشرعية مما لا يكون إلا متصفا بهذه الصفات، ففيه أن العادة لا تعتبر هنا، فإن أراد عادةَ الله في أنبيائه ؛ رجع للشرعية. الأمير والشنواني.
(۳) أي الادراك ، فهو يرجع للذكاء ، فكمال العقل والأمران بعده في كلام الشارح نفس الفطنة ، فلا معنى لذكرها هنا. الأمير والشنواني.
And what the author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned are rational conditions for prophethood (1), and its legal and customary conditions: humanity, freedom, masculinity, completeness of intellect, intelligence (2), and soundness of opinion (3).
(1) The correct view is that trustworthiness, conveying, and sagacity have a legal proof, but truthfulness in the claim of prophethood and legal rulings has a rational proof, as previously in our quotation from al-Yusi in his gloss on the commentary of al-Sanusiyya al-Kubra. So consult it. – al-Shanawani.
(2) Making intelligence one of the legal conditions, even though the author of al-Qamus defined it as quickness of understanding, is problematic regarding the rational conditions, and it has preceded that verification is that sagacity is for him? for him? because the intention is that the prophet does not have sagacity? The commentator said: among the legal conditions, and his saying: (customary) after his saying: legal, such that he is not described except with these attributes. So in it, custom is not considered here, because if he meant Allah's custom with His prophets, it returns to legal. – al-Amir and al-Shanawani.
(3) That is perception, so it returns to intelligence, so completeness of intellect and the two matters after it in the commentator's speech are the same as sagacity, so there is no meaning to mentioning them here. – al-Amir and al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the conditions of prophethood as stated by the author (Sanusi), distinguishing between rational and legal/customary conditions. The main text lists the conditions: humanity, freedom, masculinity, completeness of intellect, intelligence, and soundness of opinion. The footnotes (1–3) clarify the classification. Footnote (1) corrects the common view: trustworthiness, conveying, and sagacity are legally proven, but truthfulness in claiming prophethood and legal rulings are rationally proven. Footnote (2) debates whether intelligence (al-dhaka) is a legal or rational condition, noting that the term may overlap with sagacity (al-fatana) and that the commentator's addition of 'customary' may be redundant. Footnote (3) explains that soundness of opinion (quwwat al-ra'i) reduces to perception (al-idrak), which itself returns to intelligence, making completeness of intellect and the two subsequent terms essentially the same. The commentary thus refines the list, guarding against redundancy and clarifying the basis of each condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All conditions of prophethood are rationally necessary.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes rational conditions from legal/customary conditions; the latter are not rationally necessary but required by law and custom.

Key Takeaway

The Rational Proof for Truthfulness in Prophethood
Al-Shanawani clarifies the rational vs legal basis for specific prophethood conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that truthfulness in claiming prophethood is rationally proven, while trustworthiness, conveying, and sagacity are legally proven.

Core Idea
Not all prophethood conditions are equally evident to reason; some require divine law.

In footnote (1), al-Shanawani corrects a common misunderstanding about the basis of certain prophethood conditions. He distinguishes between conditions that reason alone can establish and those that rely on revelation. This clarification refines Sanusi's classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote text
Read al-Shanawani's footnote carefully. Notice the specific conditions he mentions.
This footnote refers back to an earlier discussion.
2
Identify the three conditions with legal proof
Al-Shanawani states that trustworthiness (amanah), conveying (tabligh), and sagacity (fatanah) are proven by divine law.
3
Identify the condition with rational proof
Truthfulness (sidq) in the claim of prophethood and in legal rulings is proven by reason alone.
4
Summarize the distinction
In your own words, explain why some conditions are rational and others legal.
Consider the role of reason vs. revelation in establishing prophethood conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره النَّاظم رحمه الله تعالى شروط عقليَّة للنُّبوَّة (١) ، وشروطها الشَّرعيّة والعادية: البشريَّة، والحرّيَّة ، والذكورة ، وكمال العقل ، والذكاء (٢) ، وقوَّة الرّأي (۳)
(١) الصواب أن الأمانة والتبليغ والفطانة دليلها شرعي ، وأما الصدق في دعوى الرسالة والأحكام الشرعية فدليله عقلي ، كما تقدم في نقلنا عن اليوسي في حاشيته على شرح كبرى السنوسي . فراجعه . الشنواني .
(۲) جعلُ الذكاء من الشروط الشرعية، مع أن صاحب القاموس فسره: بسرعة الفطنة، أنَّ من الشروط العقلية مُشكل، وقد تقدم أن التحقيق أن الفطانة معنى له ، له ، لأنَّ القصد أن النبي لا الفطانة الشارح من الشروط الشرعية ، وقول الشارح: (العادية) بعد قوله الشرعية مما لا يكون إلا متصفا بهذه الصفات، ففيه أن العادة لا تعتبر هنا، فإن أراد عادةَ الله في أنبيائه ؛ رجع للشرعية. الأمير والشنواني.
(۳) أي الادراك ، فهو يرجع للذكاء ، فكمال العقل والأمران بعده في كلام الشارح نفس الفطنة ، فلا معنى لذكرها هنا. الأمير والشنواني.
And what the author (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned are rational conditions for prophethood (1), and its legal and customary conditions: humanity, freedom, masculinity, completeness of intellect, intelligence (2), and soundness of opinion (3).
(1) The correct view is that trustworthiness, conveying, and sagacity have a legal proof, but truthfulness in the claim of prophethood and legal rulings has a rational proof, as previously in our quotation from al-Yusi in his gloss on the commentary of al-Sanusiyya al-Kubra. So consult it. – al-Shanawani.
(2) Making intelligence one of the legal conditions, even though the author of al-Qamus defined it as quickness of understanding, is problematic regarding the rational conditions, and it has preceded that verification is that sagacity is for him? for him? because the intention is that the prophet does not have sagacity? The commentator said: among the legal conditions, and his saying: (customary) after his saying: legal, such that he is not described except with these attributes. So in it, custom is not considered here, because if he meant Allah's custom with His prophets, it returns to legal. – al-Amir and al-Shanawani.
(3) That is perception, so it returns to intelligence, so completeness of intellect and the two matters after it in the commentator's speech are the same as sagacity, so there is no meaning to mentioning them here. – al-Amir and al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the conditions of prophethood as stated by the author (Sanusi), distinguishing between rational and legal/customary conditions. The main text lists the conditions: humanity, freedom, masculinity, completeness of intellect, intelligence, and soundness of opinion. The footnotes (1–3) clarify the classification. Footnote (1) corrects the common view: trustworthiness, conveying, and sagacity are legally proven, but truthfulness in claiming prophethood and legal rulings are rationally proven. Footnote (2) debates whether intelligence (al-dhaka) is a legal or rational condition, noting that the term may overlap with sagacity (al-fatana) and that the commentator's addition of 'customary' may be redundant. Footnote (3) explains that soundness of opinion (quwwat al-ra'i) reduces to perception (al-idrak), which itself returns to intelligence, making completeness of intellect and the two subsequent terms essentially the same. The commentary thus refines the list, guarding against redundancy and clarifying the basis of each condition.

Key Terms
الصدق
دليل شرعي
دليل عقلي
الأمانة
التبليغ
الفطانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All conditions of prophethood are rationally proven.
Correction: Al-Shanawani clarifies that trustworthiness, conveying, and sagacity are legally proven, not rationally.

Checkpoint
According to al-Shanawani, which condition of prophethood has a rational proof?
A) Trustworthiness
B) Conveying
C) Truthfulness in the claim of prophethood
D) Sagacity

Key Takeaway
The rational proof applies only to truthfulness in prophethood claims and rulings; other key conditions rely on revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If truthfulness in the Prophet's claim is rationally proven, have you personally examined that rational proof, or do you accept it on authority? What would you say to someone who demands that proof?
?
This lesson distinguishes between what reason can prove and what only revelation can establish. Is your trust in the Prophet's character based more on rational conviction or on cultural inheritance? How would you know the difference in your own heart?
?
If a condition like trustworthiness is only known through divine law, does that change how you view the Prophet's biography? Are you tempted to demand rational proof for every detail, or can you submit to revelation where reason is silent?

Refining the List: Overlapping Conditions of Prophethood
Critics argue that some conditions in the list are not actually distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some listed conditions in Sanusi's enumeration are redundant, as they refer to the same faculty.

Core Idea
Not every condition in the list is distinct; some like intelligence, sagacity, and soundness of opinion may overlap.

The commentators al-Amir and al-Shanawani point out that the list of legal/customary conditions includes terms that may not be distinct. Specifically, intelligence (al-dhaka') and soundness of opinion (quwwat al-ra'i) are essentially the same as sagacity (al-fatanah), and completeness of intellect (kamaal al-'aql) is also closely related. This observation refines the classification.

Guided Path
1
Read footnotes 2 and 3
Read both footnotes carefully. They are short but packed with critique.
The OCR has some unclear parts, but the main argument is discernible.
2
Identify the criticism of 'intelligence'
The commentators argue that including intelligence as a legal condition is problematic because it is defined as quickness of sagacity.
This suggests that intelligence and sagacity are not distinct.
3
Understand the redundancy of 'soundness of opinion'
They explain that soundness of opinion reduces to perception, which returns to intelligence, making it redundant with intelligence and sagacity.
4
Conclude that the list contains overlaps
Reflect on why precise terminology matters in theological definitions.
The list may have fewer distinct conditions than it appears.

Meaning of Khashyah in the Zayd Story
Sanusi clarifies 'khashyah' in the Zayd story as modesty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'khashyah' in the Quranic story of Zayd and Zaynab means modesty (al-istihya) and not fear, and how this distinction upholds the Prophet's infallibility.

Core Idea
'Khashyah' means modesty, not fear—a key to prophetic sinlessness.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary addresses a potential misunderstanding about the Prophet Muhammad's actions in the Zayd story. When Allah reproached him for 'khashyah', some might think he feared something. Sanusi clarifies that, by scholarly consensus, the term here means modesty—a subtle but crucial distinction that protects the Prophet's perfection and shows that divine reproach can be due to a station too high for worldly concerns.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection-and-answer structure
Notice how the passage is framed as a hypothetical question (fa-in qult) and a response (qult). This is a classic kalam method to clarify a difficult point.
The objector assumes 'khashyah' must mean fear, but the answer redefines it.
2
Identify key terms: khashyah, khawf, istihya
Underline these three words in the Arabic. Notice that the author explicitly distinguishes khashyah from khawf and equates it with istihya (modesty).
3
Understand why the objector is confused
The objector says: 'He did not do anything whose consequences he feared.' The context is that the Prophet knew Zaynab would be his wife, so why would he fear? This sets up the need for a different interpretation.
The objection is based on the assumption that khashyah always implies fear of consequences.
4
Grasp the scholarly consensus on khashyah
The answer says 'by agreement of the verifiers' (bi-ittifaq al-muhaqqiqin) that khashyah here means modesty. This indicates a settled theological point.
This consensus protects the Prophet from any suggestion of fear or sin.
5
Appreciate the theological nuance
Allah's reproach (mu'atabah) was not for a sin but because the Prophet showed modesty about people's slander. This reproach is a sign of his high station—only someone so elevated would be corrected for such a subtle concern.
This reinforces the doctrine of 'ismah (infallibility) by showing that even apparent criticism is actually a refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان أَعْلَمَ نبيه أنَّ زينب ستكون من أزواجه ، فلما شكاها إليه زيد قال لزيد: أمسك عليك زوجك واتق الله ، وأخفى في نفسه ما أَعلَمَهُ الله تعالى به من أنه سيتزوجها، مما الله مُبديه ومظهره بتمام التزويج وطلاق زيدٍ لها ونحوه عن الزهري، ولم يُبدِ الله من أمره إلا تزويجه إياها ، فإن قلت : فما معنى الخشية على ما قررته ؟ إذ لم يأتِ بما يخاف عواقبه، قلت: ليس معنى الخشية هنا الخوف باتفاق المحققين، وإنما معناها الاستحياء، فإنه استحيا منهم أن يقولوا: تزوج زوجة ابنه ، وتوقى المنافقين واليهود وتشنيعهم على المسلمين يقولون: تزوج زوجة ابنه بعد نهيه عن نكاح حلال الأبناء، فعاتبه الله على هذا الاستحياء، ونزهه عن الالتفات إلى أقوالهم فيما أُحلَّ له، وهذا معاتبة لعلو مقامه لا على منهي عنه . عنه. الشنواني وكذا المصنف في شرحه الكبير .
He (Allah) had informed His Prophet that Zaynab would be among his wives. So when Zayd complained to him about her, he said to Zayd: 'Keep your wife and fear Allah,' and he hid in himself what Allah had informed him of, that he would marry her, from what Allah would manifest and show with the completion of the marriage and Zayd's divorce of her and similar from al-Zuhri. And Allah did not manifest from His command except his marriage to her. If you say: So what is the meaning of 'khashyah' (fear) according to what you have established? For he did not do anything whose consequences he feared. I say: The meaning of 'khashyah' here is not fear by agreement of the verifiers, but rather its meaning is modesty (al-istihya). For he was modest before them that they might say: He married his son's wife, and he feared the hypocrites and Jews and their slander against the Muslims saying: He married his son's wife after his prohibition of marrying the wives of adopted sons. So Allah reproached him for this modesty and absolved him from paying attention to their words regarding what was made lawful for him. And this reproach is due to his high station, not regarding something forbidden. – al-Shanawani and similarly the author in his great commentary.
Source pages p. 397

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (fa-in qult... qult) that explains the term 'khashyah' (خشية) as applied to the Prophet in the context of his marriage to Zaynab. The objector questions how the Prophet could have 'fear' when he did nothing wrong. The answer clarifies that 'khashyah' here cannot mean literal fear (al-khawf) by scholarly consensus; rather it means modesty (al-istihya). The Prophet was modest about people saying he married his adopted son's wife, even though it was lawful, and he feared the slander of hypocrites and Jews. Allah reproached him for this concern about people's opinions, not for a sin, but to elevate his station above such considerations. The passage thus defends the Prophet's action and refines the understanding of the Quranic term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Prophet committed a sin or was afraid of consequences, leading to the reproach.
Correction: The passage clearly states that 'khashyah' here means modesty, not fear, and the reproach is due to his high station, not a sin.

Checkpoint

A) Fear of punishment
B) Modesty (al-istihya)
C) Anxiety about worldly affairs
D) Respect for Zayd

Key Takeaway
The reproach of the Prophet in the Zayd story was due to his modesty, not fear or sin, confirming his infallibility and high station.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you avoid doing something right because you are afraid of what others might say, is that modesty (istihya) or fear (khawf)? How can you tell the difference in your own heart, and what would it take for you to act on truth regardless of people's opinions?
?
The Prophet was reproached for being modest about a lawful matter because he paid attention to people's talk. In what area of your life are you letting the fear of slander or gossip stop you from obeying Allah fully? Will you repent and seek His pleasure over theirs starting today?
?
If you were in the Prophet's position, would you have hidden the truth to avoid awkwardness? What does this story teach you about prioritizing divine commands over social comfort, even when the command seems to risk your reputation?

Conditions of Prophethood and Childhood Possibility
Sanusi states the possibility of childhood prophethood and lists prophetic conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal conditions of prophethood and why prophethood can occur even in childhood.

Core Idea
Prophethood is possible in childhood; the proof is in the examples of Jesus and John.

This lesson establishes the foundational understanding of prophethood. Sanusi begins by stating that prophethood can occur even in childhood, citing the examples of Jesus and John (peace be upon them). He then lists the essential qualities a prophet must possess: freedom from anything that repels followers and being the most knowledgeable of the law. These conditions are universal, not contingent on age.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi presents the possibility of childhood prophethood directly.
The passage is concise but foundational.
2
Identify the two conditions
Look for the two conditions explicitly stated: freedom from repulsive traits and being the most knowledgeable of the law.
These conditions apply to all prophets.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why childhood does not conflict with these conditions. The examples of Jesus and John show that age is irrelevant.
This sets the stage for the later dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اشتراط البلوغ في النبي
ولو في الصبا(۱) ، كعيسى ويحيى الله
والسلامة عن كلِّ ما يُنفّر عن الاتِّباع حين النُّبوَّة، ومنها كونه أعلَمَ مِن جميع من بُعث إليهم بأحكام الشريعة المبعوث بها (۲) أصليّة وفرعيَّة.
مسألة اشتراط البلوغ في النبي]
واختلفوا في اشتراط البلوغ - مع اتفاقهم على جواز أنْ يبعث الله نبيًّا صغيرًا - لكنَّهم اختلفوا في الوقوع وعدمه ؛ فذهب إلى الأوَّل: الفخرُ الرَّازِيُّ (۳) ، مُستندًا لآيتي يحيى وعيسى (٤) وتأوَّلوا الآيتين على أنّهما إخبارُ عمَّا سيجب لهما حصوله ، لا عما حصل
(۳) تقدمت ترجمته .
(٤) قال تعالى في حق يحيى وَآتَيْنَهُ الْحُكم صَبيَّا :[مريم: ١٢] وقال في حق عيسى: قَالَ إِنِّي عَبْدُ اللَّهِ عَاتَنيَ الْكِتَابَ وَجَعَلَنِي نَبِيَّا [مريم: ۳۰] ويُشكل أنَّ مِن شروط التكليف البلوغ، والرّسالة تكليف، أي: إن كان شرطا في جميع الشرائع، وإلا فلا. الشنواني.
Issue: The Condition of Adulthood in the Prophet
Even in childhood, like Jesus and John (peace be upon them).
And freedom from everything that repels from following at the time of prophethood, and among them is that he be the most knowledgeable of all those to whom he was sent of the rulings of the law with which he was sent, both primary and secondary.
[Issue: The Condition of Adulthood in the Prophet]
And they differed regarding the condition of adulthood - with their agreement on the permissibility that Allah may send a prophet as a child - but they differed regarding its occurrence and non-occurrence. The first (i.e., occurrence in childhood) was held by Fakhr al-Razi (3), relying on the two verses of John and Jesus (4). And they interpreted the two verses as informing about what would necessarily be obtained for them, not about what had obtained.
(3) His biography has preceded.
(4) Allah Almighty said concerning John: 'And We gave him wisdom as a child' [Maryam: 12]; and concerning Jesus: 'He said: I am a servant of Allah; He gave me the Scripture and made me a prophet' [Maryam: 30]. And it is problematic that adulthood is among the conditions of responsibility, and prophethood is a responsibility, i.e., if it was a condition in all laws, otherwise it is not. – al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a disputed issue (مسألة) regarding whether adulthood (bulugh) is a condition for prophethood. It begins by stating that prophethood can occur even in childhood, citing Jesus and John as examples. The main text then outlines the conditions of prophethood (safety from repulsive traits and being the most knowledgeable of the law). The specific issue: scholars agreed that Allah can send a child prophet, but disagreed whether it actually occurred. Fakhr al-Razi held that it did, using Quranic verses (Maryam 12, 30). Opponents interpreted these verses as future predictions, not past events. The passage also notes the objection that responsibility (taklif) requires adulthood, and prophethood is a form of responsibility, but this may not apply to all laws. The discussion thus balances textual evidence with logical reasoning.

Key Terms
الصبا
النبوة
السلامة
الاتِّباع
الشريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What two conditions must a prophet fulfill according to the passage?
A) Adulthood and knowledge
B) Safety from repulsive traits and being most knowledgeable of the law
C) Wealth and power
D) Wisdom and bravery

Key Takeaway
Prophethood can occur in childhood, provided the prophet meets the conditions of being free from repulsive traits and possessing supreme knowledge of the law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can choose a child to be a prophet, does that challenge your assumptions about who can be used for His message? Are you limiting God based on human criteria? What will you do today to surrender your expectations to His will?
?
The prophet must be free from traits that repel others from the truth. What in your own character—pride, harshness, laziness—might push people away from the Islam you represent? What one repulsive trait will you work to remove this week?
?
The prophet must be the most knowledgeable of the law he brings. Do you take learning your religion seriously enough to be a source of correct knowledge for others? What specific step will you take today to increase your knowledge of the Quran and Sunnah?

The Dispute on Occurrence of Childhood Prophethood
Sanusi presents the disagreement on childhood prophethood and Fakhr al-Razi's proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute among scholars regarding whether prophethood has actually occurred in childhood, and the evidence used by Fakhr al-Razi.

Core Idea
Fakhr al-Razi argued childhood prophethood actually happened, using Quranic verses about John and Jesus.

While scholars agree that Allah can send a child prophet, they disagree on whether it has actually occurred. This lesson presents the view of Fakhr al-Razi, who argued for its occurrence based on two clear Quranic verses. It also discusses the counter-interpretation and the objection that prophethood, being a responsibility, requires adulthood.

Guided Path
1
Read the dispute passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the structure: agreement on possibility, disagreement on occurrence, then evidence and objection.
The passage is a classic presentation of a kalam dispute.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Identify the two verses cited by Fakhr al-Razi. Understand how they support childhood prophethood.
The verses refer to John receiving wisdom as a child and Jesus receiving Scripture and prophethood in infancy.
3
Understand the counter-interpretation
The opponents interpret the verses as predictions, not descriptions of current states. Consider why this interpretation disagrees.
This shows the role of tafsir in theological disputes.
4
Reflect on the responsibility objection
The passage raises an objection: adulthood is required for responsibility, and prophethood is a responsibility. But notes this may not be universal.
This objection is presented but not resolved in this passage.

Issue: The Condition of Adulthood in the Prophet
Even in childhood, like Jesus and John (peace be upon them).
And freedom from everything that repels from following at the time of prophethood, and among them is that he be the most knowledgeable of all those to whom he was sent of the rulings of the law with which he was sent, both primary and secondary.
[Issue: The Condition of Adulthood in the Prophet]
And they differed regarding the condition of adulthood - with their agreement on the permissibility that Allah may send a prophet as a child - but they differed regarding its occurrence and non-occurrence. The first (i.e., occurrence in childhood) was held by Fakhr al-Razi (3), relying on the two verses of John and Jesus (4). And they interpreted the two verses as informing about what would necessarily be obtained for them, not about what had obtained.
(3) His biography has preceded.
(4) Allah Almighty said concerning John: 'And We gave him wisdom as a child' [Maryam: 12]; and concerning Jesus: 'He said: I am a servant of Allah; He gave me the Scripture and made me a prophet' [Maryam: 30]. And it is problematic that adulthood is among the conditions of responsibility, and prophethood is a responsibility, i.e., if it was a condition in all laws, otherwise it is not. – al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a disputed issue (مسألة) regarding whether adulthood (bulugh) is a condition for prophethood. It begins by stating that prophethood can occur even in childhood, citing Jesus and John as examples. The main text then outlines the conditions of prophethood (safety from repulsive traits and being the most knowledgeable of the law). The specific issue: scholars agreed that Allah can send a child prophet, but disagreed whether it actually occurred. Fakhr al-Razi held that it did, using Quranic verses (Maryam 12, 30). Opponents interpreted these verses as future predictions, not past events. The passage also notes the objection that responsibility (taklif) requires adulthood, and prophethood is a form of responsibility, but this may not apply to all laws. The discussion thus balances textual evidence with logical reasoning.

Adulthood as a Condition for Prophethood
Dispute on actual childhood prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly disagreement about whether prophets can be sent as children, and how the verses about Jesus and John are interpreted.

Core Idea
Scholars agree Allah can send a child prophet, but differ on whether it actually occurred.

This lesson explores a nuanced issue in prophethood: the condition of adulthood. While all scholars agree that Allah could send a child as a prophet, they disagree on whether this has actually happened. We'll examine the two main positions and the evidence each side uses from the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two positions and the evidence cited.
The passage is concise—each word matters.
2
Identify the two positions
Fakhr al-Razi affirms that prophets were sent as children; Ibn al-'Arabi denies it. Notice how both agree on possibility but differ on actual occurrence.
3
Examine the evidence
Al-Razi uses the verses about John (19:12) and Jesus (19:30) as evidence. Ibn al-'Arabi interprets these as predictions of future wisdom, not descriptions of childhood prophecy.
This shows how the same text can support different conclusions.
4
Reflect on the interpretative principle
Think about why scholars might disagree on a scriptural interpretation. This lesson highlights that theological conclusions often depend on how one reads the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة اشتراط البلوغ في النبي ولو في الصبا، كعيسى ويحيى عليهما السلام. واختلفوا في اشتراط البلوغ - مع اتفاقهم على جواز أن يبعث الله نبيًا صغيرًا - لكنهم اختلفوا في الوقوع وعدمه. فذهب إلى الأول: الفخر الرازي، مستندًا لآيتي يحيى وعيسى. ومنعه ابن العربي. وتأولوا الآيتين على أنهما إخبار عما سيجب لهما حصوله، لا عما حصل لهما بالفعل.

Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of adulthood (bulugh) for a prophet. It begins by stating the issue: the condition is considered even if prophethood occurs in childhood, as in the cases of Jesus and John. Then it explains the disagreement: scholars agree that it is possible for God to send a child as a prophet, but they differ on whether it has actually occurred. Fakhr al-Razi argued for actual occurrence, citing the Quranic verses about John (Surah Maryam, verse 12) and Jesus (Surah Maryam, verse 30). Ibn al-'Arabi rejected this view, interpreting the verses as predictions of what would be given to them in the future, not descriptions of their state at that time.
